title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style">too many or overly lengthy quotations</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit">summarise the quotations</a>. Consider transferring direct quotations to <a href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Special:Search/Enactivism" class="extiw" title="q:Special:Search/Enactivism">Wikiquote</a> or excerpts to <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Special:Search/Enactivism" class="extiw" title="s:Special:Search/Enactivism">Wikisource</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p><b>Enactivism</b> is a position in <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a> that argues that <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a> arises through a dynamic<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (March 2025)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> interaction between an acting <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a> and its environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evan&#95;Thompson&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evan_Thompson-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It claims that the environment of an organism is brought about, or enacted, by the active exercise of that organism's sensorimotor processes. "The key point, then, is that the species brings forth and specifies its own domain of problems ...this domain does not exist "out there" in an environment that acts as a landing pad for organisms that somehow drop or parachute into the world. Instead, living beings and their environments stand in relation to each other through mutual specification or codetermination" (p.&#160;198).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Varela&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Varela-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "Organisms do not passively receive information from their environments, which they then translate into internal representations. Natural cognitive systems...participate in the generation of meaning ...engaging in transformational and not merely informational interactions: <i>they enact a world</i>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jaegher1&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaegher1-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These authors suggest that the increasing emphasis upon enactive terminology presages a new era in thinking about cognitive science.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jaegher1&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaegher1-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> How the actions involved in enactivism relate to age-old questions about <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> remains a topic of active debate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Manetti&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manetti-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The term 'enactivism' is close in meaning to 'enaction', defined as "the manner in which a subject of perception creatively matches its actions to the requirements of its situation".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tascano0&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tascano0-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The introduction of the term <i>enaction</i> in this context is attributed to  <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Varela" title="Francisco Varela">Francisco Varela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evan_Thompson" title="Evan Thompson">Evan Thompson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Rosch" title="Eleanor Rosch">Eleanor Rosch</a> in <i>The Embodied Mind</i> (1991),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tascano0&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tascano0-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-RWilson&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RWilson-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who proposed the name to "emphasize the growing conviction that cognition is not the representation of a pre-given world by a pre-given mind but is rather the enactment of a world and a mind on the basis of a history of the variety of actions that a being in the world performs".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Varela&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Varela-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was further developed by Thompson and others,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evan&#95;Thompson&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evan_Thompson-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to place emphasis upon the idea that experience of the world is a result of mutual interaction between the sensorimotor capacities of the organism and its environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RWilson&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RWilson-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, some writers maintain that there remains a need for some degree of the mediating function of representation in this new approach to the science of the mind.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rowlands&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rowlands-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The initial emphasis of enactivism upon sensorimotor skills has been criticized as "cognitively marginal",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ClarkA&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkA-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but it has been extended to apply to higher level cognitive activities, such as social interactions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jaegher1&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaegher1-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "In the enactive view,... knowledge is constructed: it is constructed by an agent through its sensorimotor interactions with its environment, co-constructed between and within living species through their meaningful interaction with each other. In its most abstract form, knowledge is co-constructed between human individuals in socio-linguistic interactions...Science is a particular form of social knowledge construction...[that] allows us to perceive and predict events beyond our immediate cognitive grasp...and also to construct further, even more powerful scientific knowledge."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rohde&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rohde-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Enactivism is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Situated_cognition" title="Situated cognition">situated cognition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Embodied_cognition" title="Embodied cognition">embodied cognition</a>, and is presented as an alternative to <a href="/wiki/Cognitivism_(psychology)" title="Cognitivism (psychology)">cognitivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Computationalism">computationalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_dualism" class="mw-redirect" title="Cartesian dualism">Cartesian dualism</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Philosophical_aspects">Philosophical aspects</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Philosophical aspects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Enactivism is one of  a cluster of related theories sometimes known as the <i>4Es, also known as <a href="/wiki/4E_cognition" title="4E cognition">4E cognition</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gallagher&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As described by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Rowlands" title="Mark Rowlands">Mark Rowlands</a>, mental processes are:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Embodied</b> involving more than the brain, including a more general involvement of bodily structures and processes.</li>
<li><b>Embedded</b> functioning only in a related external environment.</li>
<li><b>Enacted</b> involving not only neural processes, but also things an organism <i>does</i>.</li>
<li><b>Extended</b> into the organism's environment.</li></ul>
<p>Enactivism proposes an alternative to <a href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">dualism</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, in that it emphasises the interactions between mind, body and the environment, seeing them all as inseparably intertwined in mental processes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EThompson&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EThompson-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The self arises as part of the process of an embodied entity interacting with the environment in precise ways determined by its physiology.   In this sense, individuals can be seen to "grow into" or arise from their interactive role with the world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Burman&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burman-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>"Enaction is the idea that organisms create their own experience through their actions. Organisms are not passive receivers of input from the environment, but are actors in the environment such that what they experience is shaped by how they act."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hutchins&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchins-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>In <i>The Tree of Knowledge</i> Maturana &amp; Varela proposed the term <i>enactive</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maturana&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maturana-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "to evoke the view of knowledge that what is known is brought forth, in contraposition to the more classical views of either cognitivism<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cognitivism&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cognitivism-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>Note 1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">connectionism</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Connectionism&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connectionism-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>Note 2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They see enactivism as providing a middle ground between the two extremes of <a href="/wiki/Representationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Representationalism">representationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solipsism" title="Solipsism">solipsism</a>. They seek to "confront the problem of understanding how our existence-the <a href="/wiki/Praxis_(process)" title="Praxis (process)">praxis</a> of our living- is coupled to a surrounding world which appears filled with regularities that are at every instant the result of our biological and social histories.... to find a <i>via media</i>: to understand the regularity of the world we are experiencing at every moment, but without any point of reference independent of ourselves that would give certainty to our descriptions and cognitive assertions.  Indeed the whole mechanism of generating ourselves, as describers and observers tells us that our world, as the world which we bring forth in our coexistence with others, will always have precisely that mixture of regularity and mutability, that combination of solidity and shifting sand, so typical of human experience when we look at it up close."[<i>Tree of Knowledge</i>, p.&#160;241] Another important notion relating to enactivism is autopoiesis. The word refers to a system that is able to reproduce and maintain itself. Maturana &amp; Varela describe that "This was a word without a history, a word that could directly mean what takes place in the dynamics of the autonomy proper to living systems"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maturana,&#95;Humberto&#95;R.,&#95;1928–1980&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maturana,_Humberto_R.,_1928–1980-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Using the term autopoiesis, they argue that any closed system that has autonomy, self-reference and self-construction (or, that has autopoietic activities) has cognitive capacities. Therefore, cognition is present in all living systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maturana,&#95;Humberto&#95;R.,&#95;1928–1980&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maturana,_Humberto_R.,_1928–1980-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This view is also called autopoietic enactivism.
</p><p>Radical enactivism is another form of enactivist view of cognition. Radical enactivists often adopt a thoroughly non-representational, enactive account of basic cognition. Basic cognitive capacities mentioned by Hutto and Myin include  perceiving, imagining and remembering.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hutto,&#95;Daniel&#95;D&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutto,_Daniel_D-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schlicht&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlicht-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They argue that those forms of basic cognition can be explained without positing mental representations. With regard to complex forms of cognition such as language, they think mental representations are needed, because there needs explanations of content. In human being's public practices, they claim that "such intersubjective practices and sensitivity to the relevant norms comes with the mastery of the use of public symbol systems" (2017, p.&#160;120), and so "as it happens, this appears only to have occurred in full form with construction of sociocultural cognitive niches in the human lineage" (2017, p.&#160;134).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hutto,&#95;Daniel&#95;D&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutto,_Daniel_D-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They conclude that basic cognition as well as cognition in simple organisms such as bacteria are best characterized as non-representational.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hutto,&#95;Daniel&#95;D&#95;18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutto,_Daniel_D-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schlicht&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlicht-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Enactivism also addresses the <a href="/wiki/Hard_problem_of_consciousness" title="Hard problem of consciousness">hard problem of consciousness</a>, referred to by Thompson as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Explanatory_gap" title="Explanatory gap">explanatory gap</a></i> in explaining how consciousness and subjective experience are related to brain and body.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EThompson2&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EThompson2-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  "The problem with the dualistic concepts of consciousness and life in standard formulations of the hard problem is that they exclude each other by construction".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EThompson3&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EThompson3-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Instead, according to Thompson's view of enactivism, the study of consciousness or <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">phenomenology</a> as exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Husserl" class="mw-redirect" title="Husserl">Husserl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merleau-Ponty" class="mw-redirect" title="Merleau-Ponty">Merleau-Ponty</a> is to complement science and its objectification of the world. "The whole universe of science is built upon the world as directly experienced, and if we want to subject science itself to rigorous scrutiny and arrive at a precise assessment of its meaning and scope, we must begin by reawakening the basic experience of the world of which science is the second-order expression" (Merleau-Ponty, <i>The phenomenology of perception</i> as quoted by Thompson, p.&#160;165). In this interpretation, enactivism asserts that science is formed or enacted as part of humankind's interactivity with its world, and by embracing phenomenology "science itself is properly situated in relation to the rest of human life and is thereby secured on a sounder footing."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EThompson4&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EThompson4-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baldwin&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Enaction has been seen as a move to conjoin <a href="/wiki/Representationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Representationalism">representationalism</a> with <a href="/wiki/Phenomenalism" title="Phenomenalism">phenomenalism</a>, that is, as adopting a <a href="/wiki/Constructivist_epistemology" class="mw-redirect" title="Constructivist epistemology">constructivist epistemology</a>, an epistemology centered upon the active participation of the subject in constructing reality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mutelesi&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mutelesi-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chiari&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiari-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, 'constructivism' focuses upon more than a simple 'interactivity' that could be described as a minor adjustment to 'assimilate' reality or 'accommodate' to it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Glaserfeld&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glaserfeld-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Constructivism looks upon interactivity as a radical, creative, revisionist process in which the knower <i>constructs</i> a personal 'knowledge system' based upon their experience and tested by its viability in practical encounters with their environment. Learning is a result of perceived anomalies that produce dissatisfaction with existing conceptions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Glasersfeld2&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glasersfeld2-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Shaun_Gallagher" title="Shaun Gallagher">Shaun Gallagher</a> also points out that <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatism</a> is a forerunner of enactive and extended approaches to cognition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gallagher&#95;110–126&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher_110–126-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to him, enactive conceptions of cognition can be found in many pragmatists such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sanders_Peirce" title="Charles Sanders Peirce">Charles Sanders Peirce</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>. For example, Dewey says that "The brain is essentially an organ for effecting the reciprocal adjustment to each other of the stimuli received from the environment and responses directed upon it" (1916, pp.&#160;336–337).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This view  is fully consistent with enactivist arguments that cognition is not just a matter of brain processes and brain is one part of the body consisting of the dynamical regulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gallagher&#95;110–126&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher_110–126-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Brandom" title="Robert Brandom">Robert Brandom</a>, a neo-pragmatist, comments that "A founding idea of pragmatism is that the most fundamental kind of intentionality (in the sense of directedness towards objects) is the practical involvement with objects exhibited by a sentient creature dealing skillfully with its world" (2008, p.&#160;178).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>How does constructivism relate to enactivism? From the above remarks it can be seen that <a href="/wiki/Ernst_von_Glasersfeld" title="Ernst von Glasersfeld">Glasersfeld</a> expresses an interactivity between the knower and the known quite acceptable to an enactivist, but does not emphasize  the structured probing of the environment by the knower that leads to the "perturbation relative to some expected result" that then leads to a new understanding.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Glasersfeld2&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glasersfeld2-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is this probing activity, especially where it is not accidental but deliberate, that characterizes enaction, and invokes <i>affect</i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ward2&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that is, the motivation and planning that lead to doing and to fashioning the probing, both observing and modifying the environment, so that "perceptions and nature condition one another through generating one another."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Diettrich&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diettrich-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The questioning nature of this probing activity is not an emphasis of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Piaget" title="Jean Piaget">Piaget</a> and Glasersfeld.
</p><p>Sharing enactivism's stress upon both action and embodiment in the incorporation of knowledge, but giving Glasersfeld's mechanism of viability an <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_evolution" title="Introduction to evolution">evolutionary</a> emphasis,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Diettrich2&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diettrich2-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_epistemology" title="Evolutionary epistemology">evolutionary epistemology</a>. Inasmuch as an organism must reflect its environment well enough for the organism to be able to survive in it, and to be competitive enough to be able to reproduce at sustainable rate, the structure and reflexes of the organism itself embody knowledge of its environment. This biology-inspired theory of the growth of knowledge is closely tied to <a href="/wiki/Universal_Darwinism" title="Universal Darwinism">universal Darwinism</a>, and is associated with evolutionary epistemologists such as <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donald_T._Campbell" title="Donald T. Campbell">Donald T. Campbell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Munz" title="Peter Munz">Peter Munz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Cziko" title="Gary Cziko">Gary Cziko</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gontier&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gontier-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to Munz, "an organism is an <i>embodied theory</i> about its environment... Embodied theories are also no longer expressed in language, but in anatomical structures or reflex responses, etc."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gontier&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gontier-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Munz&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munz-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One objection to enactive approaches to cognition is the so-called "scale-up objection". According to this objection, enactive theories only have limited value because they cannot "scale up" to explain more complex cognitive capacities like human thoughts. Those phenomena are extremely difficult to explain without positing representation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> But recently, some philosophers are trying to respond to such objection. For example, Adrian Downey (2020) provides a non-representational account of Obsessive-compulsive disorder, and then argues that ecological-enactive approaches can respond to the "scaling up" objection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Psychological_aspects">Psychological aspects</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Psychological aspects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>McGann &amp; others<sup id="cite&#95;ref-McGann&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGann-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> argue that enactivism attempts to mediate between the explanatory role of the coupling between cognitive agent and environment and the traditional emphasis on brain mechanisms found in neuroscience and psychology.  In the interactive approach to social cognition developed by De Jaegher &amp;  others,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gallagher0&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher0-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gallager1&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallager1-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ratcliffe&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ratcliffe-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the dynamics of interactive processes are seen to play significant roles in coordinating interpersonal understanding, processes that in part include what they call <a href="#Participatory_sense-making"><i>participatory sense-making</i></a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeJaeger0&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeJaeger0-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeJaegher1&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeJaegher1-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent developments of enactivism in the area of social neuroscience involve the proposal of <i>The Interactive Brain Hypothesis</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-DiPaolo3&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiPaolo3-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where social cognition brain mechanisms, even those used in non-interactive situations, are proposed to have interactive origins.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Enactive_views_of_perception">Enactive views of perception</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Enactive views of perception"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the enactive view, perception "is not conceived as the transmission of information but more as an exploration of the world by various means. Cognition is not tied into the workings of an 'inner mind', some cognitive core, but occurs in directed interaction between the body and the world it inhabits."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-McGann2&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGann2-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alva_No%C3%AB" title="Alva Noë">Alva Noë</a> in advocating an enactive view of perception<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Noe&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noe-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> sought to resolve how we perceive three-dimensional objects, on the basis of two-dimensional input.  He argues that we perceive this solidity (or 'volumetricity') by appealing to patterns of sensorimotor expectations. These arise from our agent-active  'movements and interaction' with objects, or 'object-active' changes in the object itself. The solidity is perceived through our expectations and skills in knowing how the object's appearance would change with changes in how we relate to it. He saw all perception as an active exploration of the world, rather than being a passive process, something which happens to us.
</p><p>Noë's idea of the role of 'expectations' in three-dimensional perception has been opposed by several philosophers, notably by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Clark" title="Andy Clark">Andy Clark</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ClarkA1&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkA1-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Clark points to difficulties of the enactive approach.  He points to internal processing of visual signals, for example, in the ventral and dorsal pathways, <a href="/wiki/Two-streams_hypothesis" title="Two-streams hypothesis">the two-streams hypothesis</a>. This results in an integrated perception of objects (their recognition and location, respectively) yet this processing cannot be described as an action or actions. In a more general criticism, Clark suggests that perception is not a matter of expectations about sensorimotor mechanisms guiding perception. Rather, although the limitations of sensorimotor mechanisms constrain perception, this sensorimotor activity is drastically filtered to fit current needs and purposes of the organism, and it is these imposed 'expectations' that govern perception, filtering for the 'relevant' details of sensorimotor input (called "sensorimotor summarizing").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ClarkA1&#95;49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkA1-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These sensorimotor-centered and purpose-centered views appear to agree on the general scheme but disagree on the dominance issue – is the dominant component peripheral or central. Another view, the closed-loop perception one, assigns equal a-priori dominance to the peripheral and central components. In closed-loop perception, perception emerges through the process of inclusion of an item in a motor-sensory-motor loop, i.e., a loop (or loops) connecting the peripheral and central components that are relevant to that item.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AhiAssa1&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AhiAssa1-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The item can be a body part (in which case the loops are in steady-state) or an external object (in which case the loops are perturbed and gradually converge to a steady state). These enactive loops are always active, switching dominance by the need.
</p><p>Another application of enaction to perception is analysis of the human hand. The many remarkably demanding uses of the hand are not learned by instruction, but through a history of engagements that lead to the acquisition of skills. According to one interpretation, it is suggested that "the hand [is]...an organ of cognition", not a faithful subordinate working under top-down instruction, but a partner in a "bi-directional interplay between manual and brain activity."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hutto&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutto-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Hutto" title="Daniel Hutto">Daniel Hutto</a>: "Enactivists are concerned to defend the view that our most elementary ways of engaging with the world and others - including our basic forms of perception and perceptual experience - are mindful in the sense of being phenomenally charged and intentionally directed, despite being non-representational and content-free."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hutto2&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutto2-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hutto calls this position 'REC' (<u>R</u>adical <u>E</u>nactive <u>C</u>ognition): "According to REC, there is no way to distinguish neural activity that is imagined to be genuinely content involving (and thus truly mental, truly cognitive) from other non-neural activity that merely plays a supporting or enabling role in making mind and cognition possible."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hutto2&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutto2-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Participatory_sense-making">Participatory sense-making</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Participatory sense-making"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hanne_De_Jaegher&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hanne De Jaegher (page does not exist)">Hanne De Jaegher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ezequiel_Di_Paolo" title="Ezequiel Di Paolo">Ezequiel Di Paolo</a> (2007)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeJaeger0&#95;44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeJaeger0-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> have extended the enactive concept of sense-making<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EThompson3&#95;22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EThompson3-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> into the social domain. The idea takes as its departure point the process of interaction between individuals in a social encounter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeJaegher&#95;etal&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeJaegher_etal-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> De Jaegher and Di Paolo argue that the interaction process itself can take on a form of autonomy (operationally defined). This allows them to define social cognition as the generation of meaning and its transformation through interacting individuals.
</p><p>The notion of participatory sense-making has led to the proposal that interaction processes can sometimes play constitutive roles in social cognition (De Jaegher, Di Paolo, Gallagher, 2010).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeJaegher1&#95;45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeJaegher1-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been applied to research in <a href="/wiki/Social_neuroscience" title="Social neuroscience">social neuroscience</a><i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-DiPaolo3&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiPaolo3-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-SchilbachTimmermans&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SchilbachTimmermans-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></i> and <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autism</a>.<i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeJaegher&#95;autism&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeJaegher_autism-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></i>
</p><p>In a similar vein, "an inter-enactive approach to agency holds that the behavior of agents in a social situation unfolds not only according to their individual abilities and goals, but also according to the conditions and constraints imposed by the autonomous dynamics of the interaction process itself".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-STorrance&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-STorrance-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to Torrance, enactivism involves five interlocking themes related to the question "What is it to be a (cognizing, conscious) agent?" It is:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-STorrance&#95;56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-STorrance-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>1. to be a biologically autonomous (<a href="/wiki/Autopoiesis" title="Autopoiesis">autopoietic</a>) organism</dd>
<dd>2. to generate <i>significance</i> or <i>meaning</i>, rather than to act via...updated internal representations of the external world</dd>
<dd>3. to engage in sense-making via dynamic coupling with the environment</dd>
<dd>4. to 'enact' or 'bring forth' a world of significances by mutual co-determination of the organism with its enacted world</dd>
<dd>5. to arrive at an experiential awareness via lived embodiment in the world.</dd></dl>
<p>Torrance adds that "many kinds of agency, in particular the agency of human beings, cannot be understood separately from understanding the nature of the interaction that occurs between agents." That view introduces the social applications of enactivism. "Social cognition is regarded as the result of a special form of action, namely <i>social interaction</i>...the enactive approach looks at the circular dynamic within a dyad of embodied agents."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FuchsT&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FuchsT-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Cultural_psychology" title="Cultural psychology">cultural psychology</a>, enactivism is seen as a way to uncover cultural influences upon feeling, thinking and acting.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Verheggen&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verheggen-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Baerveldt and Verheggen argue that "It appears that seemingly natural experience is thoroughly intertwined with sociocultural realities." They suggest that the social patterning of experience is to be understood through enactivism, "the idea that the reality we have in common, and in which we find ourselves, is neither a world that exists independently from us, nor a socially shared way of representing such a pregiven world, but a world itself brought forth by our ways of communicating and our joint action....The world we inhabit is manufactured of 'meaning' rather than 'information'.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baerveldt&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baerveldt-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Niklas_Luhmann" title="Niklas Luhmann">Luhmann</a> attempted to apply Maturana and Varela's notion of autopoiesis to social systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Luhmann&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Luhmann-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "A core concept of social systems theory is derived from biological systems theory: the concept of <i>autopoiesis</i>. Chilean biologist Humberto Maturana come up with the concept to explain how biological systems such as cells are a product of their own production." "Systems exist by way of operational closure and this means that they each construct themselves and their own realities."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moeller&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moeller-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Educational_aspects">Educational aspects</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Educational aspects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The first definition of enaction was introduced by psychologist <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Bruner" title="Jerome Bruner">Jerome Bruner</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pugliese&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pugliese-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beck&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beck-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who introduced enaction as 'learning by doing' in his discussion of how children learn, and how they can best be helped to learn.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bruner&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruner-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bruner2&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruner2-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He associated enaction with two other ways of knowledge organization: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">Iconic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">Symbolic</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bruner3&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruner3-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>"Any domain of knowledge (or any problem within that domain of knowledge) can be represented in three ways: by a set of actions appropriate for achieving a certain result (enactive representation); by a set of summary images or graphics that stand for a concept without defining it fully  (iconic representation); and by a set of symbolic or logical propositions drawn from a symbolic system that is governed by rules or laws for forming and transforming propositions (symbolic representation)"</dd></dl>
<p>The term 'enactive framework' was elaborated upon by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Varela" title="Francisco Varela">Francisco Varela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humberto_Maturana" title="Humberto Maturana">Humberto Maturana</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bopry&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bopry-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sriramen argues that enactivism provides "a rich and powerful explanatory theory for learning and being."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sriraman&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sriraman-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and that it is closely related to both the <a href="/wiki/Piaget%27s_theory_of_cognitive_development" title="Piaget&#39;s theory of cognitive development">ideas of cognitive development</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Piaget" title="Jean Piaget">Piaget</a>, and also the <a href="/wiki/Social_constructivism" title="Social constructivism">social constructivism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vygotsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Vygotsky">Vygotsky</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sriraman&#95;68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sriraman-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Piaget focused on the child's immediate environment, and suggested cognitive structures like spatial perception emerge as a result of the child's interaction with the world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roth&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roth-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to Piaget, children <i>construct</i> knowledge, using what they know in new ways and testing it, and the environment provides feedback concerning the adequacy of their construction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cziko&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cziko-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a cultural context, Vygotsky suggested that the kind of cognition that can take place is not dictated by the engagement of the isolated child, but is also a function of social interaction and dialogue that is contingent upon a sociohistorical context.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kincheloe&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kincheloe-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Enactivism in educational theory "looks at each learning situation as a <a href="/wiki/Complex_system" title="Complex system">complex system</a> consisting of teacher, learner, and context, all of which frame and co-create the learning situation."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Vithal&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vithal-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Enactivism in education is very closely related to <a href="/wiki/Situated_cognition" title="Situated cognition">situated cognition</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-VanDeGevel&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanDeGevel-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which holds that "knowledge is situated, being in part a product of the activity, context, and culture in which it is developed and used."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Collins&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This approach challenges the "separating of what is learned from how it is learned and used."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Collins&#95;74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Artificial_intelligence_aspects">Artificial intelligence aspects</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Artificial intelligence aspects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Enactive_interfaces" title="Enactive interfaces">Enactive interfaces</a></div>
<p>The ideas of enactivism regarding how organisms engage with their environment have interested those involved in <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_robotics" title="Cognitive robotics">robotics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human%E2%80%93computer_interaction" title="Human–computer interaction">man-machine interfaces</a>. The analogy is drawn that a robot can be designed to interact and learn from its environment in a manner similar to the way an organism does,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sandini&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandini-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a human can interact with a computer-aided design tool or data base using an interface that creates an enactive environment for the user, that is, all the user's tactile, auditory, and visual capabilities are enlisted in a mutually explorative engagement, capitalizing upon all the user's abilities, and not at all limited to cerebral engagement.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bordegoni&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bordegoni-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In these areas it is common to refer to <a href="/wiki/Affordance" title="Affordance">affordances</a> as a design concept, the idea that an environment or an interface affords opportunities for enaction, and good design involves optimizing the role of such affordances.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Norman&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norman-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kim&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kim-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kaipainen&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaipainen-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Boy&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boy-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thannhuber&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thannhuber-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The activity in the AI community has influenced enactivism as a whole. Referring extensively to modeling techniques for <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_robotics" title="Evolutionary robotics">evolutionary robotics</a> by Beer,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beer&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beer-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the modeling of learning behavior by Kelso,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kelso&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kelso-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and to modeling of sensorimotor activity by Saltzman,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Saltzman&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saltzman-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> McGann, De Jaegher, and Di Paolo discuss how this work makes the dynamics of coupling between an agent and its environment, the foundation of enactivism, "an operational, empirically observable phenomenon."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-McGann3&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGann3-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That is, the AI environment invents examples of enactivism using concrete examples that, although not as complex as living organisms, isolate and illuminate basic principles.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mathematical_formalisms">Mathematical formalisms</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mathematical formalisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">artificial general intelligence</a></div>
<p>Enactive cognition has been formalised in order to address subjectivity in <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">artificial general intelligence</a>.
</p><p>A mathematical formalism of AGI is an agent proven to maximise a measure of intelligence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Prior to 2022, the only such formalism was <a href="/wiki/AIXI" title="AIXI">AIXI</a>, which maximised “the ability to satisfy goals in a wide range of environments”.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2015 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jan_Lieke&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jan Lieke (page does not exist)">Jan Lieke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Hutter" title="Marcus Hutter">Marcus Hutter</a> showed that "Legg-Hutter intelligence is measured with respect to a fixed UTM. AIXI is the most intelligent policy if it uses the same UTM", a result which "undermines all existing optimality properties for AIXI", rendering them subjective.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Criticism">Criticism</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Enactivism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Criticism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One of the essential theses of this approach is that biological systems generate meanings, i.e. they are <a href="/wiki/Semiotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Semiotic">semiotic</a> systems, engaging in transformational and not merely informational interactions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jaegher1&#95;3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaegher1-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since this thesis raised the problems of beginning cognition for organisms in the developmental stage of only simple reflexes (the <a href="/wiki/Binding_problem" title="Binding problem">binding problem</a> and the problem of primary data entry<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), enactivists proposed the concept of embodied information that serves to start cognition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evan&#95;Thompson&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evan_Thompson-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, critics highlight that this idea requires introducing the nature of <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a> before engaging embodied information.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Val&#95;Theory&#95;2023&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Val_Theory_2023-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a natural environment, the stimulus-reaction pair (causation) is unpredictable due to many irrelevant stimuli claiming to be randomly associated with the embodied information.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Val&#95;Theory&#95;2023&#95;91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Val_Theory_2023-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While embodied information is only beneficial when intentionality is already in place, enactivists introduced the notion of the generation of meanings by biological systems (engaging in transformational interactions) without introducing a neurophysiological basis of intentionality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Val&#95;Theory&#95;2023&#95;91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Val_Theory_2023-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

Academic. p.&#160;234 <i>ff</i>. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1845400705" title="Special:BookSources/978-1845400705"><bdi>978-1845400705</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+14%3A+The+crucial+role+of+haptic+perception&amp;rft.btitle=Artificial+Consciousness&amp;rft.pages=234+%27%27ff%27%27&amp;rft.pub=Academic&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-1845400705&amp;rft.au=Pietro+Morasso&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.google.com%2Fsearch%3Fq%3Disbn%3A1845400704&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEnactivism" class="Z3988"></span></li>

<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFGeorge-Louis&#95;BaronEric&#95;BruillardChristophe&#95;Dansac1999" class="citation web cs1">George-Louis Baron; Eric Bruillard; Christophe Dansac (January 1999). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://halshs.archives-ouvertes.fr/docs/00/00/18/64/PDF/REPRDel1.pdf">"Educational Multimedia Task Force – MM 1045, REPRESENTATION"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Educational+Multimedia+Task+Force+%E2%80%93+MM+1045%2C+REPRESENTATION&amp;rft.date=1999-01&amp;rft.au=George-Louis+Baron&amp;rft.au=Eric+Bruillard&amp;rft.au=Christophe+Dansac&amp;rft&#95;id=http%3A%2F%2Fhalshs.archives-ouvertes.fr%2Fdocs%2F00%2F00%2F18%2F64%2FPDF%2FREPRDel1.pdf&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEnactivism" class="Z3988"></span> An overview of the rationale and means and methods for the study of representations that the learner constructs in his/her attempt to understand knowledge in a given field.
